"a-ciyatta ","","","","{\em a + tyakta} (Bollée 1994a: 207; if this etymology is correct, {\em tyakta} may here have the active meaning of ‘having left out, omitted;not having done', viz., cooking especially for a monk or nun. The {\em locus classicus} may be  Dasav 5,1,17 a-ciyattaṁ na pavise, ciyattaṁ pavise kulaṁ ‘he shouldnot go to a family that did cook especially for him’ -- food which he wasnot allowed to accept and for which reason he could consider those people “unkind” to him --, ‘but he should go where one had abstaïned therefrom’ so as to enable him to take what was offered as being pure. Haribhadra comments this passage by {\em a-prīti-kulaṁ yatra praviśadbhiḥ sādhubhir a-prītir utpadyate na ca nivārayanti kutaścin nimittântarāt. Etad api na praviśet tat-saṁklésa-nimittatva-prasaṅgāt. Ciyattam [...] praviśet kulaṁ tad-anugraha-prasaṅgāt}. Perhaps a-ciyatta, too, should thus be listed among the negative expressions of the {\em a-hiṁsā} type. Charpentier's difficulty [1922: 320] is partly due to his wrong reading a-vyatta at Utt 11, 9. As ciyatta is rare compared to its negative one may assume that many people tried to improve their {\em karman} by meansnot sanctioned by the monastic rules)","","","1264 (cty: {\em a-prītika}), 1268, 1394, 1489, 1579-81, 3707, 5242 etc."

"a-saī̆","","","","{\em a + sati}  loc. sg. masc. of {\em sat}  ‘in the absence of’ (c. gen.). Cf. Schubring 1963: 236; Bollée 1994a: 132; frequently as a-saī ya (often written as -e) for which expletive addition cf. chac ca\ ? Thus, e.g., 1464, 1620, 1726, 2332, 3193 etc. Where the metre requires a-saīe I can only assume analogy or case contamination . PSM assumes a feminine {\em deśī} noun asaï related to {\em asattva} and  explained by a-bhāva. I have left a-saïe where a syllable is anceps.   Adelheid Mette in a p.c. proposes to consider it a loc. of a-saï ~ {\em a-sak̥rt} used as an adjective","","","1078, 1502, 2076, 2942, 2987 et passim","ifc. annâ°","VavBh ud. 6: 283"

¹eraṇḍa  ts. ‘Ricinus communis L.’ (Meyer 1937: I 103sq.); -- 216 (cty: {\em eraṇḍa-druma}), 1029; -- ĀvNHar 957
²eraṇḍa  [d.] ‘rabid’ (perhaps abbreviation of a cpd. denoting the affection with hydrophobia, agaïnst which branches of the castor oil tree  have perhaps been used as mentioned by Meyer, l.c. [see prec.]); -- 3817 (cty: {\em haḍakkita}); -- ifc. aṇ-°
eraṇḍaïya  [d.] ‘made rabid’ (?); -- 2926 (cty: {\em haḍakkayita}, which is not found in MW, but cf. CDIAL, 13942. This is obviously a Sanskritization of Gujarātī haḍkāyo ‘(a dog etc.) affected by rabies'. Guj. haḍakvā means ‘the disease haḍak due to unnatural disturbance/imbalance of the wind element (vā) according to the Āyurvedic system [Bhayani, p.c. of 10.12.1995]. Cf. Suśruta, Kalpasthāna 7,7 dealing with {\em jala-trāsa} ‘hydrophobia’)

guṇṭha  [d.]\ (DŚK; Desin 2,91. As g. in Prakrit and ghoṭaka in Pā. mean ‘a bad horse’ it is perhaps related to Muṇḍa words meaning ‘limping’ or ‘cripple’ [Kuiper 1948: 53]). sutta  {\em s√kta} (?\ Wright 1966: 7 quoted in Bollée 1977: 31; Oberlies 1995: 141 with older literature; one should also think of the exclamation “sādhu, sādhu” after a Buddhist dhamma-kathā and perhaps of the $\epsilon$$\upsilon$$\alpha$$\gamma$$\gamma$$\epsilon$$\lambda$$\iota$$\o$$\nu$. To decide the etymology of sutta between {\em sūtra} and {\em s√kta} is hardly possible, but {\em s√kta} is not the only brahmanical notion adopted by the “heretics".
